Lord Advocate</a></i> as <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Session" title="Court of Session">Lord President of the Court of Session</a>, he stated, "The principle of unlimited sovereignty of Parliament is a distinctively English principle and has no counterpart in Scottish constitutional law." He continued, "Considering that the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1707">Union legislation</a> extinguished the Parliaments of Scotland and England and replaced them by a new Parliament, I have difficulty in seeing why the new Parliament of Great Britain must inherit all the peculiar characteristics of the English Parliament but none of the Scottish." Nevertheless, he did not give a conclusive opinion on the subject.\n</p><p>Thus, the question of Parliamentary sovereignty appears to remain unresolved. Parliament has not passed any Act defining its own sovereignty. A related possible limitation on Parliament relates to the Scottish legal system and Presbyterian faith, preservation of which were Scottish preconditions to the creation of the unified Parliament. Since the Parliament of the United Kingdom was set up in reliance on these promises, it may be that it has no power to make laws that break them.\n</p><p>Parliament\'s power has often been eroded by its own Acts. Acts passed in <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland_Act_1921" title="Church of Scotland Act 1921">1921</a> and 1925 granted the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> complete independence in ecclesiastical matters. More recently, its power has been restricted by membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, which has the power to make laws enforceable in each member state. In the <a href="/wiki/Factortame_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Factortame case">Factortame case</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> ruled that British courts could have powers to overturn British legislation contravening European law.\n</p><p>Parliament has also created national devolved parliaments and assemblies with differing degrees of legislative authority in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>. Parliament still has the power over areas for which responsibility lies with the devolved institutions, but would gain the agreement of those institutions to act on their behalf. Similarly, it has granted the power to make regulations to Ministers of the Crown, and the power to enact religious legislation to the <a href="/wiki/General_Synod" title="General Synod">General Synod</a> of the Church of England. (Measures of the General Synod and, in some cases proposed <a href="/wiki/Statutory_instrument" title="Statutory instrument">statutory instruments</a> made by ministers, must be approved by both Houses before they become law.)\n</p><p>In every case aforementioned, authority has been conceded by Act of Parliament and may be taken back in the same manner. It is entirely within the authority of Parliament, for example, to abolish the devolved governments in Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland or to leave the EU. However, Parliament also revoked its legislative competence over Australia and Canada with the <a href="/wiki/Australia_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Australia Act">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canada_Act_1982" title="Canada Act 1982">Canada Acts</a>: although the Parliament of the United Kingdom could pass an Act reversing its action, it would not take effect in Australia or Canada as the competence of the <b>Imperial Parliament</b> is no longer recognised there in law.\n</p><p>One well-recognised exception to Parliament\'s power involves binding future Parliaments. No Act of Parliament may be made secure from amendment or repeal by a future Parliament. For example, although the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union 1800</a> states that the Kingdoms of Great Britain and Ireland are to be united "forever", Parliament permitted southern Ireland to leave the United Kingdom in 1922.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Privileges">Privileges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Privileges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_privilege" title="Parliamentary privilege">Parliamentary privilege</a></div>\n<p>Each House of Parliament possesses and guards various ancient privileges. The House of Lords relies on inherent right. In the case of the House of Commons, the Speaker goes to the Lords\' Chamber at the beginning of each new Parliament and requests representatives of the Sovereign to confirm the Lower House\'s "undoubted" privileges and rights. The ceremony observed by the House of Commons dates to the reign of King Henry VIII. Each House is the guardian of its privileges, and may punish breaches thereof. The extent of parliamentary privilege is based on law and custom. Sir William Blackstone states that these privileges are "very large and indefinite", and cannot be defined except by the Houses of Parliament themselves.\n</p><p>The foremost privilege claimed by both Houses is that of <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a> in debate; nothing said in either House may be questioned in any court or other institution outside Parliament. Another privilege claimed is that of freedom from arrest; at one time this was held to apply for any arrest except for <a href="/wiki/High_treason" title="High treason">high treason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Felony" title="Felony">felony</a> or <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_the_peace" title="Breach of the peace">breach of the peace</a> but it now excludes any arrest on criminal charges; it applies during a session of Parliament, and 40 days before or after such a session.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Members of both Houses are no longer privileged from service on <a href="/wiki/Jury" title="Jury">juries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both Houses possess the power to punish breaches of their privilege. Contempt of Parliament\xe2\x80\x94for example, disobedience of a <a href="/wiki/Subpoena" title="Subpoena">subpoena</a> issued by a committee\xe2\x80\x94may also be punished. The House of Lords may imprison an individual for any fixed period of time, but an individual imprisoned by the House of Commons is set free upon <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_session#Prorogation" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary session">prorogation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The punishments imposed by either House may not be challenged in any court, and the Human Rights Act does not apply.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until at least 2015, members of the House of Commons also had the privilege of a separate seating area in the Palace of Westminster canteen, protected by a false partition labelled "MPs only beyond this point", so that they did not have to sit with canteen staff taking a break. This provoked mockery from a newly elected 20-year-old MP who described it as "ridiculous" snobbery.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emblem">Emblem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Emblem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parliament_portcullis.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/47/Parliament_portcullis.png/140px-Parliament_portcullis.png" width="140" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/47/Parliament_portcullis.png/210px-Parliament_portcullis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/47/Parliament_portcullis.png/280px-Parliament_portcullis.png 2x" data-file-width="289" data-file-height="345" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Parliament_portcullis.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Crowned <a href="/wiki/Portcullis" title="Portcullis">Portcullis</a> of Parliament</div></div></div>\n<p>The quasi-official emblem of the Houses of Parliament is a crowned <a href="/wiki/Portcullis" title="Portcullis">portcullis</a>. The portcullis was originally the badge of various English noble families from the 14th century. It went on to be adopted by the kings of the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor dynasty</a> in the 16th century, under whom the Palace of Westminster became the regular meeting place of Parliament. The crown was added to make the badge a specifically royal symbol.\n</p><p>The portcullis probably first came to be associated with the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a> through its use as decoration in the rebuilding of the Palace after the fire of 1512. However, at the time it was only one of many symbols. The widespread use of the portcullis throughout the Palace dates from the 19th century, when <a href="/wiki/Charles_Barry" title="Charles Barry">Charles Barry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Pugin" title="Augustus Pugin">Augustus Pugin</a> used it extensively as a decorative feature in their designs for the new Palace built following the disastrous 1834 fire.\n</p><p>The crowned portcullis came to be accepted during the 20th century as the emblem of both houses of parliament. This was simply a result of custom and usage rather than a specific decision. The emblem now appears on official stationery, publications and papers, and is stamped on various items in use in the Palace of Westminster, such as cutlery, silverware and china.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Various shades of red and green are used for visual identification of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See title="Commonwealth Parliamentary Association">Commonwealth Parliamentary Association</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2"><div><sup><small>1</small></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">European Union law</a> applies in the United Kingdom and <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_invocation_of_Article_50_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union" title="United Kingdom invocation of Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union">invokation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_from_the_European_Union" title="Withdrawal from the European Union">Article 50</a>, the United Kingdom is <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">set to leave</a> the European Union. A <a href="/wiki/European_Union_(Withdrawal)_Act_2018" title="European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018">Great Repeal Bill</a> has been proposed to incorporate all <i>current</i> European laws into <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Law of the United Kingdom">British law</a>.\n<br /><sup><small>2</small></sup> "Bailiwick-wide" legislature passed in the <a href="/wiki/States_of_Guernsey" title="States of Guernsey">States of Guernsey</a> applies not only in <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">Guernsey</a>, but also in <a href="/wiki/Alderney" title="Alderney">Alderney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sark" title="Sark">Sark</a>, with the consent of their governments.</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox"
